<span id="freeTextContainer8027816115038210692">It is 1979. Gas lines wrap around the block and there are hostages in Iran. I was in high school in 1979 and the setting is interesting to me because it is so real. It made me remember much that I had forgotten.<br/><br/>Rabbit is older though not more mature. He is still a poor husband, father, friend, and boss. Worse, he is still a poor human. And yet the book is a good read. Updike works magic, making me care about the story even though I dislike the characters.</span>

<div id="review_1504746374" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1504746374" />
    <a title="Tim" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/37256808-tim"><img alt="Tim" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1444088588p2/37256808.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1504746374?book_show_action=true">Jan 10, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tim" href="/user/show/37256808-tim">Tim</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/37256808-tim?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/37256808-tim?shelf=america">america</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1504746374" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7401776682084527477">This is the third installment in Updike's celebrated tetralogy about the life of Harold "Rabbit" Angstrom, a regular American guy living a regular American life. Told in that uniquely graceful prose that was the key to Updike's brilliance, this series stands out as a set of memorable portraits of a place (Pennsylvania), and an average man who lives there and deals with the changes in his world and in himself. The first Rabbit book showed us a young man bucking against the conformist world of the</span>
  <span id="freeText7401776682084527477" style="display:none">This is the third installment in Updike's celebrated tetralogy about the life of Harold "Rabbit" Angstrom, a regular American guy living a regular American life. Told in that uniquely graceful prose that was the key to Updike's brilliance, this series stands out as a set of memorable portraits of a place (Pennsylvania), and an average man who lives there and deals with the changes in his world and in himself. The first Rabbit book showed us a young man bucking against the conformist world of the late 1950s and early 1960s. In the second, Rabbit tumbles headlong into the turmoil of the late 1960s and early 1970s. In this we see the America of 1979-1980, when the wild experimentation has receded, and Rabbit is settling down into a comfortable middle age. He and his wife are happily reunited, the disasters of the past have been forgiven, and the Toyota dealership is doing well with Rabbit at the helm.<br /><br />Of course there are still things nagging at him. Ever the sensualist, Rabbit continues to be attracted to women other than Janet. He wonders what happened to the child he and his paramour conceived in "Rabbit, Redux." His son Nelson is a thorn in his side, and Rabbit is less than gracious and willing when the boy expresses a desire to leave college and come work for the family business. In fact, much of the drama that takes place in the book is Nelson's drama, seen thru his father's eyes. There is some infidelity and longing too (as one would expect in an Updike story), but this is more a book about manhood and family than about romance. Some people have accused Updike's writing of being too vague, too hazy in terms of story and subject matter, but in this there is, if not a riveting plot, a series of developments and changes in a family's life over the course of about a year.<br /><br />But ah, that prose. That was Updike's genius - those scintillating, graceful, perceptive descriptions. At one point he describes a pretty ordinary experience - looking out the window of an airplane as it comes in for a landing - and brings it to life with vivid, rhythmic prose. Mix that ability with a wry, subtle humor and an eager eye that looked carefully at American life and American families. The perspective is ultimately a male one, and one that regards women with some lack of empathy. I couldn't help thinking that Rabbit may have been what Updike himself avoided becoming, what he could have been if he hadn't left Pennsylvania and gone off to Harvard and become a Great American Writer (whatever became of those, btw?) Updike succeeds in creating a believable portrayal of a guy running a car dealership (he obviously did some research on that), and he also brings in details from the time, such as news stories and descriptions of places and buildings that add to the realism. <br /><br />Imo this was a great novel, one that deserved the awards that it won, and that should have a place in the bookcase of anyone who loves great American writing. When people seek out stories that present a believable, humorous, intelligent, and wonderfully written look at what life was like in 1979 or so, this belongs on the list.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7401776682084527477" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_579779599" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/579779599" />
    <a title="Drew" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1200912-drew"><img alt="Drew" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1566807838p2/1200912.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/579779599?book_show_action=true">Apr 03, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Drew" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Drew" href="/user/show/1200912-drew">Drew</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer579779599" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12206699225977215443">Both <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/85386.Rabbit__Run' title='Rabbit, Run by John Updike' rel='nofollow noopener'>Rabbit, Run</a> and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/46917.Rabbit_Redux' title='Rabbit Redux by John Updike' rel='nofollow noopener'>Rabbit Redux</a> are powerful and engaging novels that cut against the grain of their decades: in the former we see Updike, an anti-Kerouac, introducing Harry Angstrom, an anti-Sal Paradise who doesn't go on the road, who refuses the new freedoms and stays put; in the latter Updike graduates to an anti-Kesey, chronicling Angstrom's nervous flirtation with -and final rejection of - the new liberties that the sixties have opened up. Rabbit is Rich takes us forward another decade t</span>
  <span id="freeText12206699225977215443" style="display:none">Both <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/85386.Rabbit__Run" title="Rabbit, Run by John Updike" rel="nofollow noopener">Rabbit, Run</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/46917.Rabbit_Redux" title="Rabbit Redux by John Updike" rel="nofollow noopener">Rabbit Redux</a> are powerful and engaging novels that cut against the grain of their decades: in the former we see Updike, an anti-Kerouac, introducing Harry Angstrom, an anti-Sal Paradise who doesn't go on the road, who refuses the new freedoms and stays put; in the latter Updike graduates to an anti-Kesey, chronicling Angstrom's nervous flirtation with -and final rejection of - the new liberties that the sixties have opened up. Rabbit is Rich takes us forward another decade to the end of the seventies, and Harry Angstrom's mid-forties. The novel is baggier, looser and less shapely, and so is Harry: middle age has given him a paunch and a penchant for dreamy rumination that gives the book a dreamy, half-stoned air. <br />Like the decade whose tail-end it covers, the book revels in the brash and the ugly, in a world of cowardly compromise and stretch-pant prosperity. As such it paints a grotesque but convincing picture of the middle-aged Angstrom, a man who has come to wealth through the good offices of his father-in-law and who, at 45 lives in his mother-in-law's house. Here Updike is marvellously contrarian, giving the finger to both the liberalism that was spawned by the 60s and to the American mythos of the self made man. <br />Despite these interesting aspects the book finally fails to live up to its predecessors: Carter, the Iran hostage situation, oil prices, wife swapping and the tribulations of home ownership are simply a weaker sort of material, and they make for a novel that's not only baggy, at times it frays and feels as if it might tear apart at any moment.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12206699225977215443" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_732309061" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/732309061" />
    <a title="Patrick" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5847899-patrick"><img alt="Patrick" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1450211590p2/5847899.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/732309061?book_show_action=true">Oct 01, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

<span id="freeTextContainer13327098746703345938">I'm slowly working my way through the <i>Rabbit</i> books and, where <i>Redux</i> felt like a bit of a mis-step, <i>Rabbit is Rich</i> works rather better.   <span id="freeText13327098746703345938" style="display:none">I'm slowly working my way through the <i>Rabbit</i> books and, where <i>Redux</i> felt like a bit of a mis-step, <i>Rabbit is Rich</i> works rather better. Maybe it's simply the benefit of having had Harry Angstrom in my head for two books already by now, but here he comes across as a more convincing, fully developed character than he did in, particularly, the second book. And while I wasn't around at the time, I thought Updike's evocation of the mood of the time more convincing than in the previous novel. <br /><br />The relationship between Angstrom and his son, Nelson is to my mind what the book is centred upon. The particular discomfort of two men seeing their own failings reflected in the other. <br /><br />There are as always with Updike, some beautifully written passages (though I could do without the golf, being of a mind with Mark Twain on that sport) and where <i>Redux</i> had a lot of bad sex writing, <i>Rich</i> could more fairly be described as having a fair amount of writing about bad sex. </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/146587-ashley?shelf=mybasicbookshelf">mybasicbookshelf</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2555503" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14794412394104647149">i love updike---i started reading the rabbit books and then got so fully into rabbit that i went through the series pretty quickly. <br/><br/>i liked updike's first in the series, "rabbit run," but it took me a while to really love him as much as i did by the time i got to "rabbit is rich". i love the roundness of his stories and his patience in letting his characters develop slowly. updike pays attention to the details of everyday life without making them of monumental importance. but after a while you s</span>
  <span id="freeText14794412394104647149" style="display:none">i love updike---i started reading the rabbit books and then got so fully into rabbit that i went through the series pretty quickly. <br /><br />i liked updike's first in the series, "rabbit run," but it took me a while to really love him as much as i did by the time i got to "rabbit is rich". i love the roundness of his stories and his patience in letting his characters develop slowly. updike pays attention to the details of everyday life without making them of monumental importance. but after a while you see that he has carefully built the development of the story to account for the small decisions, habits, and fantasies of his characters without pushing them forward before the groundwork has been laid. this means that a story that can feel very loosely constructed at first ultimately proves to be a well- and efficiently-constructed story. that said, i'm never ready for where he takes me and i know i start to love his characters--none of whom make themselves easily lovable--because i resent the bad things that sometimes happen to them. <br /><br />updike is modern, in my view, in the sense that a lot of the work in these books seems to me to be asking about the possibilities of love and human connection---he is very careful to avoid sentimentality. so much so that the loneliness of his character can start to become difficult to read. there's a sense of characters who are compartmentalized and isolated through the sheer force of modern ego rather than for any meaningful or existential reason. and there are only moments when real connection is possible---or, maybe more accurately, it's only clear at moment in the texts that the love connections between family members (since this is a series about a man and his family) have been sustained over time even if love is rarely expressed in anything more direct than a muffled and awkward moment. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="14794412394104647149" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_155942017" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/155942017" />
    <a title="Jee Koh" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/113906-jee-koh"><img alt="Jee Koh" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1456688836p2/113906.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/155942017?book_show_action=true">Mar 22, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jee Koh" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jee Koh" href="/user/show/113906-jee-koh">Jee Koh</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer155942017" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14923823938738107831">What's extraordinary about Updike's art is how ordinary his materials are. No sensational subjects like pederasty, pandemic or terrorist plot, although <i>Rabbit Is Rich</i> teases with the possibility of incest. There is couple swapping, on a vacation at the Bahamas, its treatment is, however, neither moralistic nor voyeuristic, but sympathetic about human desires and fears. No epiphanic event: Pru, Harry Angstrom's daughter-in-law, falls from the stairs, but keeps her baby. She does not change, and n</span>
  <span id="freeText14923823938738107831" style="display:none">What's extraordinary about Updike's art is how ordinary his materials are. No sensational subjects like pederasty, pandemic or terrorist plot, although <i>Rabbit Is Rich</i> teases with the possibility of incest. There is couple swapping, on a vacation at the Bahamas, its treatment is, however, neither moralistic nor voyeuristic, but sympathetic about human desires and fears. No epiphanic event: Pru, Harry Angstrom's daughter-in-law, falls from the stairs, but keeps her baby. She does not change, and neither does her feckless husband, Nelson. <br /><br />Instead of sensation or epiphany, Updike offers in this third installment of his Rabbit tetralogy, the lived experience of a Toyota salesman in 1979, who has become rich because of his wife, who is struggling against the decay of age, who feels constantly threatened by a sullen son, and who reads <i>Consumer Reports</i> with a seriousness devoted to the Bible in an earlier age. <br /><br />The last trait is a clue to the extraordinary nature of ordinary Harry. Despite his moral failings, his perceptual denseness, his linguistic crudities, he is immensely attractive, to readers and women, because of his great <i>hope</i> for life. This hope, so Protestant in spirit, is satirized in the feisty comical scenes of Harry's speculations in gold and silver. It is also castigated in the plot involving Ruth, a past lover, and her daughter, both of whom Harry abandoned. The meeting between Ruth and Harry is one of the most poignant scenes in my reading. <br /><br />Against death, necessity and boredom, however, this hope lifts Harry to some greater place. Sullied, doubtful, compromised, this may be the only kind of transcendence possible in our postmodern age, but its smallness only makes it more to be cherished. </span>
  <a data-text-id="14923823938738107831" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/550228086?book_show_action=true">Mar 02, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dan" href="/user/show/1109495-dan">Dan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer550228086" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14892095567144500374">I loved-loved-loved this book. In my mind it's a masterpiece, and the only question is whether it's excellence rises enough to compare to Gatsby and The Sun Also Rises. I'll say it's not quite there, but it's close. Harry Angstrom has always been a great character, but in this book Nelson and Janice finally find their voice. What elevates this book is the battle between Rabbit and Nelson, backed up by his mom and grandmother. The kid is coddled and spoiled, and annoying as hell, but Updike gives</span>
  <span id="freeText14892095567144500374" style="display:none">I loved-loved-loved this book. In my mind it's a masterpiece, and the only question is whether it's excellence rises enough to compare to Gatsby and The Sun Also Rises. I'll say it's not quite there, but it's close. Harry Angstrom has always been a great character, but in this book Nelson and Janice finally find their voice. What elevates this book is the battle between Rabbit and Nelson, backed up by his mom and grandmother. The kid is coddled and spoiled, and annoying as hell, but Updike gives him enough vulnerability to make him more than a caricature. Rabbit finally has the adult voice Updike should have given him Rabbit Redux, but let's just call that regrettable book a '60s indiscretion. <br /><br />Updike has always frustrated me because of his tendency to over-orchestrate his characters and his penchant for showing off what a good writer he is. In this novel he hardly has any false steps. The tension between the characters drive the story and Updike stays out of the way. What I thought was a false note, Rabbit's simple-minded lusting for a friend's wife, turns out to simply be a setup for a crucial scene.<br /><br />Perhaps I'm so enthralled with this book because I share a similar age with Rabbit - he's 46 and I'm 45. Certainly I can't think of a book that so ably gets into the head of a 40-something as well as this one. I think this simply reflects that after 20 years Updike finally understood all the nuances to Harry, his great invention. <br /><br />Now I'm going to start Rabbit At Rest, weighing in at 22 hours. Arthur Morey's brilliant narration throughout all the Rabbit books has s been a joy. I've gotten quite a few stares on the sidewalk and on the train as I laugh out loud at Harry's observations.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14892095567144500374" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_48687080" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/48687080" />
    <a title="Stefani" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1358347-stefani"><img alt="Stefani" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1548133961p2/1358347.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/48687080?book_show_action=true">Mar 09, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stefani" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stefani" href="/user/show/1358347-stefani">Stefani</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer48687080" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15955558851894524012">I had no idea what to expect from John Updike. I picked up this book on a whim at the library after hearing about his death, hoping that there was a shred of something in this story that I could relate to. Turns out there wasn't, but John Updike is a gifted writer, in my opinion, and manages to infuse an unremarkable industrial town in Pennsylvania with the light of a thousand ships, illuminating every detail in eye-popping color. I guess the 70's were supposed to be the decade of sexual experim</span>
  <span id="freeText15955558851894524012" style="display:none">I had no idea what to expect from John Updike. I picked up this book on a whim at the library after hearing about his death, hoping that there was a shred of something in this story that I could relate to. Turns out there wasn't, but John Updike is a gifted writer, in my opinion, and manages to infuse an unremarkable industrial town in Pennsylvania with the light of a thousand ships, illuminating every detail in eye-popping color. I guess the 70's were supposed to be the decade of sexual experimentation; throughout the novel, Updike's characters are having the kind of sweaty, orgasmic, kinky sex that, if portrayed on film, might be shown in a seedy theater on the wrong side of town. I could literally hear the 70's porn beat pulsating at each interval. Rabbit, the main character, is a despicable character, his sagging and floppy body a steady reminder that his glory days as a basketball player are long gone. Largely a figurehead running his wife's family's car dealership, he spends an inordinate amount of time fantasizing about his wife's friends, people who come into the dealership,etc...cue porn soundtrack....There is a lot of allusion to affairs of the past, dysfunctional relationships, and hardship, yet the family is still living together in the same household, chained to the past. When Rabbit's son, Nelson, returns from college, Rabbit finds his small world turned upside down and he must adjust to his changing environment. Although this was set in the 70's, and there are frequent cultural references to the decade, there are many parallels to the economic times we are currently living in. </span>
  <a data-text-id="15955558851894524012" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<ul>
<li class='cover' id='bookCover_11494'>
<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11494.Humboldt_s_Gift"><img alt="Humboldt&#39;s Gift" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/books/1388954094l/11494._SX98_.jpg" />
</a>

  
<script type="text/javascript" charset="utf-8">
//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('y/lMP6Y2e70cq6FwpKSjmUzg8nBjP8OARcA+X2QPff5tVAPVZYeywAigrYm+lDnH9Hd5UCtLmPonNmESrxG5IQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('Ryak/ZImPxlYggwH307o3KsIlOZhqECmC+8TKcghO/vhi+sXUZf2ZEyJAP7FfnKCE58fxincG9xpGUxkAz//JA==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/6878.John_Updike">About John Updike</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer9440548900010562015">John Hoyer Updike was an American writer. Updike's most famous work is his Rabbit series (Rabbit, Run; Rabbit Redux; Rabbit Is Rich; Rabbit At Rest; and Rabbit Remembered). Rabbit is Rich and Rabbit at Rest both won Pulitzer Prizes for Updike. Describing his subject as "the American small town, Protestant middle class," Updike is well known for his careful craftsmanship and prolific writing, havin</span>
  <span id="freeText9440548900010562015" style="display:none">John Hoyer Updike was an American writer. Updike's most famous work is his Rabbit series (Rabbit, Run; Rabbit Redux; Rabbit Is Rich; Rabbit At Rest; and Rabbit Remembered). Rabbit is Rich and Rabbit at Rest both won Pulitzer Prizes for Updike. Describing his subject as "the American small town, Protestant middle class," Updike is well known for his careful craftsmanship and prolific writing, having published 22 novels and more than a dozen short story collections as well as poetry, literary criticism and children's books. Hundreds of his stories, reviews, and poems have appeared in The New Yorker since the 1950s. His works often explore sex, faith, and death, and their inter-relationships.<br /><br />He died of lung cancer at age 76.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9440548900010562015" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
